<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Recent African origin of modern humans</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about modern humans. For migrations of early humans, see <a href="/wiki/Early_hominin_expansions_out_of_Africa" title="Early hominin expansions out of Africa">Early hominin expansions out of Africa</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map-of-human-migrations.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="889" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Map-of-human-migrations.jpg/450px-Map-of-human-migrations.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Map-of-human-migrations.jpg/675px-Map-of-human-migrations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/37/Map-of-human-migrations.jpg 2x" width="450"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map-of-human-migrations.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the migration of modern humans out of Africa, based on <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a>. Colored rings indicate thousand years before present.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>recent African origin of modern humans</b>, also called the "<b>Out of Africa</b>" <b>theory</b> (<b>OOA</b>), <b>recent single-origin hypothesis</b> (<b>RSOH</b>), <b>replacement hypothesis</b>, or <b>recent African origin model</b> (<b>RAO</b>), is, in <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">paleoanthropology</a>, the dominant model of the geographic origin and <a href="/wiki/Early_human_migrations" title="Early human migrations">early migration</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>). The model proposes a single area of origin for modern humans. The <i>single-origin hypothesis</i> is cited as having the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a> as of the mid-2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16826514Quo_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16826514Quo-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12802315_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12802315-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to this model, modern humans evolved in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> between 400,000 and 200,000 years ago, and then began to disperse throughout the world roughly 70,000 to 135,000 years ago. As of 2011, it appears likely that there were two waves of migration out of Africa.</p>
<p>The first wave took place between 130,000–115,000 years ago via northern Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21273486_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21273486-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21212332_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21212332-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21601174_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21601174-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17372199_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17372199-6">[6]</a></sup> and appears to have mostly died out or retreated (although there is some evidence of a presence of modern humans in China about 80,000 years ago).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu2015_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liu2015-7">[7]</a></sup> In the 2010s, <a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">genomic testing</a> of living populations has located <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">archaic admixture</a> of modern humans outside of Africa with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denisovans" title="Denisovans">Denisovans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pruf13comal_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-pruf13comal-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>A second dispersal took place after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toba_supereruption" title="Toba supereruption">Toba supereruption</a> (ca. 77,000 years ago) via the so-called Southern Route, following the <a href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">southern coastline of Asia</a>, which led to the lasting colonization of Australia by around 65,000-50,000 years ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[note 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McChesney2015_short_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-McChesney2015_short-12">[note 2]</a></sup> while Europe was populated by an early offshoot which settled the Near East and Europe less than 55,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung_McChesney2015_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung_McChesney2015-13">[11]</a></sup>}}<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macaulay2005_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macaulay2005-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Posth2016_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Posth2016-15">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>An archaic view of hominid evolution, <a href="/wiki/Polygenism" title="Polygenism">Polygenism</a>, holds that different human peoples had differing origins. Many religious texts refer to this concept. From 1984 to 2003, an alternate scientific hypothesis was the <a href="/wiki/Multiregional_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Multiregional origin of modern humans">multiregional origin of modern humans</a>, which envisioned a wave of <i>Homo sapiens</i> migrating earlier from Africa and interbreeding with local <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> populations in varied regions of the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JurmainKilgore2008_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-JurmainKilgore2008-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10766948_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10766948-17">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="African_origins_of_Homo_sapiens">African origins of <i>Homo sapiens</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: African origins of Homo sapiens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">Anatomically modern humans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_Homo_sapiens" title="Archaic Homo sapiens">Archaic Homo sapiens</a></div>
<div class="toccolours searchaux" id="Container" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">

<div class="toccolours" id="Legend" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div>
<div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#One_dimension" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">millions of years ago</a>.<br/>
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">Life timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison_en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="555" data-file-width="910" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison_en.png/220px-Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison_en.png/330px-Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison_en.png/440px-Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison_en.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sapiens_neanderthal_comparison_en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Anatomical comparison of the skulls of a modern human (left) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_neanderthalensis" title="Homo neanderthalensis">Homo neanderthalensis</a></i> (right).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">Pan</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a>) genera of <a href="/wiki/Hominini" title="Hominini">Hominini</a> <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee%E2%80%93human_last_common_ancestor" title="Chimpanzee–human last common ancestor">diverged</a> between 4 and 13 million years ago, in Africa.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">Anatomically modern humans</a> had originated in Africa by about 200,000 years ago, documented by the find of the <a href="/wiki/Omo_remains" title="Omo remains">Omo remains</a> in south-western Ethiopia. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeo_2005_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_2005-18">[16]</a></sup> There is strong evidence that <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens sapiens">Homo sapiens sapiens</a></i> coexisted with other <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphologically</a> distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominids</a> at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeo_2005_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_2005-18">[16]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_idaltu" title="Homo sapiens idaltu">Homo sapiens idaltu</a></i>, found at site <a href="/wiki/Middle_Awash" title="Middle Awash">Middle Awash</a> in Ethiopia, lived about 160,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2017, fossils attributed to <i>H. sapiens,</i> along with stone tools, dated to approximately 300,000 years ago, were unearthed at Jebel Irhoud, in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SN_2017_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-SN_2017-20">[18]</a></sup> These finds have facial features in common with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">Anatomically modern humans</a> and distinct from <i>H. Erectus</i>, while the shape of the brain case remains to be determined. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SN_2017_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-SN_2017-20">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also in 2017, <a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">genomic testing</a> has produced results related to population divergences of <i>H.Sapiens</i> groups within Africa, as detailed below in the section <i>Genetic reconstruction - Mitochondrial haplogroups - Within Africa.</i> The <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San people</a> of Southern Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Sandawe_people" title="Sandawe people">Sandawe people</a> of East Africa branched off between 260,000 and 350,000 years ago and have remained relatively genetically isolated since then. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_2017_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_2017-21">[19]</a></sup> The San people are a subgroup of the <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> peoples.</p>
<p>The trend in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cranial_capacity" title="Cranial capacity">cranial expansion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">acheulean</a> elaboration of stone tool technologies which occurred between 400,000 years ago and the second interglacial period in the Middle <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> (around 250,000 years ago) provide evidence for a transition from <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> to <i>H. sapiens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DelsonTattersall2000_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-DelsonTattersall2000-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning about 100,000 years ago, evidence of more sophisticated technology and artwork begins to emerge and by 50,000 years ago, fully <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">modern behaviour</a> becomes more prominent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeo_2005_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo_2005-18">[16]</a></sup> Stone tools show regular patterns that are reproduced or duplicated with more precision while <a href="/wiki/Bone_tool" title="Bone tool">tools made of bone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antler" title="Antler">antler</a> appear for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker_2009_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker_2009-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tattersall_2009_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tattersall_2009-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_finds_outside_Africa">Fossil finds outside Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Fossil finds outside Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fossils of early <i>Homo sapiens</i> were found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qafzeh" title="Qafzeh">Qafzeh</a> cave in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and have been dated to 80,000 to 100,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu.2C_Prugnolle_et_al.2006_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu.2C_Prugnolle_et_al.2006-25">[23]</a></sup> A fossil of a modern human dated to 54,700 years ago was found in <a href="/wiki/Manot_Cave" title="Manot Cave">Manot Cave</a> in Israel, named <a href="/wiki/Manot_1" title="Manot 1">Manot 1</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> though the dating was questioned by <a href="#CITEREFGroucutt_et_al.2015">Groucutt et al. (2015)</a>. Fossils from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mungo_Lake_remains" title="Mungo Lake remains">Lake Mungo, Australia</a>, have been dated to about 42,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doisj.quascirev.2005.07.022_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-doisj.quascirev.2005.07.022-28">[26]</a></sup> Other fossils from a site called Madjedbebe have been dated to at least 65,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StFleur2017_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-StFleur2017-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tianyuan_man" title="Tianyuan man">Tianyuan man</a> from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> has a probable date range between 38,000 and 42,000 years ago, while <a href="/wiki/Liujiang_man" title="Liujiang man">Liujiang man</a> from the same region has a probable date range between 67,000 and 159,000 years ago. According to 2013 DNA tests, Tianyuan man is related "to many present-day <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-daily_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-daily-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> Tianyuan is similar in <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> to <a href="/wiki/Minatogawa_Man" title="Minatogawa Man">Minatogawa Man</a>, modern humans dated between 17,000 and 19,000 years ago and found on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okinawa" title="Okinawa">Okinawa</a> Island, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersals_out_of_Africa">Dispersals out of Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dispersals out of Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_human_migrations" title="Early human migrations">Early human migrations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="851" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg/320px-Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg/480px-Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg/640px-Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg.png 2x" width="320"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of <a href="/wiki/Early_human_migrations" title="Early human migrations">early human migrations</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup><br/>
1. <i>Homo sapiens</i><br/>
2. Neanderthals<br/>
3. Early hominins</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">Anatomically modern humans</a> originated in Africa by about 200,000 years ago. The accepted theory is that there were two dispersals from African populations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macaulay2005_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Macaulay2005-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyin2011_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyin2011-37">[35]</a></sup> an early one via northern Africa which left traces in some human populations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> and a second one which populated the entire world.</p>
<p>The first dispersal took place between 130,000–115,000 years ago via northern Africa, but died out or retreated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21273486_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21273486-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21212332_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21212332-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21601174_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21601174-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17372199_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17372199-6">[6]</a></sup> Chinese researchers question this extinction, claiming that modern humans were present in China already 80,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu2015_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Liu2015-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>A second dispersal took place via the so-called Southern Route, either before<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012-39">[37]</a></sup> or after<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macaulay2005_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Macaulay2005-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Posth2016_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Posth2016-15">[13]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba event</a>, which happened between 69,000 and 77,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012-39">[37]</a></sup> This dispersal followed the southern coastline of Asia, crossing about 250 kilometres (155 mi) of sea, and colonized Australia by around 65,000-50,000 years ago. According to this theory, Europe was populated either by a migration out of India, which was repopulated from southeast Asia after the Toba-event (pre-Toba hypothesis), or by an early offshoot which settled the Near East and Europe (post-Toba hypothesis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macaulay2005_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Macaulay2005-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Posth2016_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Posth2016-15">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nonetheless, in July 2017, evidence suggests that <i>Homo sapiens</i> may have migrated from Africa as early as 270,000 years ago, much earlier than the 70,000 years ago thought previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170704_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170704-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC-20170704_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC-20170704-41">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_northern_Africa_dispersal">Early northern Africa dispersal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early northern Africa dispersal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The early northern Africa dispersal took place between 130,000–115,000 years ago.</p>
<p>The hypothesized departure of humankind from Africa to colonise the rest of the world involved them crossing the Straits of Bab el Mandab in the southern <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and moving along the green coastlines around Arabia and thence to the rest of Eurasia. Beginning 135,000 years ago, tropical Africa experienced <a href="/wiki/Megadrought" title="Megadrought">megadroughts</a> which drove the humans from the land and towards the sea shores, and forced them to cross over to other continents. The researchers used radiocarbon dating techniques on pollen grains trapped in lake-bottom mud to establish vegetation over the ages of the <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> lake in Africa, taking samples at 300-year-intervals. Samples from the megadrought times had little pollen or charcoal, suggesting sparse vegetation with little to burn. The area around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Malawi" title="Lake Malawi">Lake Malawi</a>, today heavily forested, was a desert approximately 135,000 to 90,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U_of_AZ_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-U_of_AZ-42">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The discovery of stone tools in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> in 2011 indicated the presence of modern humans at least 100,000 and 125,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21273486_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21273486-3">[3]</a></sup> leading to a resurgence of the "long-neglected" North African route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21212332_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21212332-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21601174_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21601174-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17372199_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17372199-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/History_of_Oman" title="History of Oman">Oman</a>, a site was discovered by Bien Joven in 2011 containing more than 100 surface scatters of stone tools belonging to the late Nubian Complex, known previously only from <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_record" title="Archaeological record">archaeological excavations</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>. Two optically stimulated luminescence age estimates place the Arabian Nubian Complex at approximately 106,000 years old. This provides evidence for a distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone_age" title="Stone age">stone age</a> technocomplex in southern Arabia, around the earlier part of the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Isotope_Stage_5" title="Marine Isotope Stage 5">Marine Isotope Stage 5</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fossils of early <i>Homo sapiens</i> were found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qafzeh" title="Qafzeh">Qafzeh</a> cave in Israel and have been dated 80,000 to 100,000 years ago. These humans seem to have either become extinct or retreated back to Africa 70,000 to 80,000 years ago, possibly replaced by southbound Neanderthals escaping the colder regions of ice-age Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinlayson200968_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinlayson200968-45">[43]</a></sup> Hua Liu <i>et al.</i> analyzed autosomal microsatellite markers dating to about 56,000 years ago. They interpret the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleontological" title="Paleontological">paleontological</a> fossil as an isolated early offshoot that retracted back to Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu.2C_Prugnolle_et_al.2006_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu.2C_Prugnolle_et_al.2006-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Kuhlwilm and his co-authors, <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a> <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">contributed to modern humans genetically</a> around 100,000 years ago, from humans which split off from other modern humans around 200,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAB-EastNeandertals_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAB-EastNeandertals-46">[44]</a></sup> They found that "the ancestors of Neanderthals from the Altai Mountains and early modern humans met and interbred, possibly in the Near East, many thousands of years earlier than previously thought".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAB-EastNeandertals_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-DAB-EastNeandertals-46">[44]</a></sup> According to co-author Ilan Gronau, "This actually complements archaeological evidence of the presence of early modern humans out of Africa around and before 100 ka by providing the first genetic evidence of such populations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAB-EastNeandertals_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-DAB-EastNeandertals-46">[44]</a></sup> Similar <a href="/wiki/Genetic_admixture" title="Genetic admixture">genetic admixture</a> events have been <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">noted in other regions</a> as well. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denovisans_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denovisans-47">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In China, the <a href="/wiki/Liujiang_man" title="Liujiang man">Liujiang man</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">柳江人</span>) is among the earliest modern <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> found in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liujiang_hominid_site_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-liujiang_hominid_site-48">[46]</a></sup> Liujiang man is a <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens sapiens">Homo sapiens sapiens</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49">[47]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[48]</a></sup> The date most commonly attributed to the remains is 67,000 BP. High rates of variability yielded by various dating techniques carried out by different researchers place the most widely accepted range of dates with 67,000 BP as a minimum, but does not rule out dates as old as 159,000 BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49">[47]</a></sup> <a href="#CITEREFLiu.2C_Martin.C3.B3n-Torres_et_al.2015">Liu, Martinón-Torres et al. (2015)</a> claim that modern human teeth have been found in China dating to at least 80,000 years ago.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Route_dispersal">Southern Route dispersal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Southern Route dispersal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">Coastal migration</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_route">Coastal route</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Coastal route">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_Sea2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="426" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Red_Sea2.png/220px-Red_Sea2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Red_Sea2.png/330px-Red_Sea2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Red_Sea2.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_Sea2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> crossing</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By some 70,000 years ago, a part of the bearers of mitochondrial haplogroup <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L3_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L3 (mtDNA)">L3</a> migrated from <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>. It has been estimated that from a population of 2,000 to 5,000 individuals in Africa, only a small group, possibly as few as 150 to 1,000 people, crossed the Red Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> The group that crossed the Red Sea travelled along the coastal route around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> to India, which appears to be the first major settling point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15339343_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15339343-53">[51]</a></sup> <a href="#CITEREFWells2003">Wells (2003)</a> argued for the route along the southern coastline of Asia, across about 250 kilometres (155 mi), reaching Australia by around 50,000 years ago.</p>
<p>Today at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb_straits" title="Bab-el-Mandeb straits">Bab-el-Mandeb straits</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> is about 20 kilometres (12 mi) wide but 50,000 years ago sea levels were 70 m (230 ft) lower (owing to glaciation) and the water was much narrower. Though the straits were never completely closed, they were narrow enough and there may have been islands in between to have enabled crossing using simple rafts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyin2011_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyin2011-37">[35]</a></sup> Shell <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middens" title="Middens">middens</a> 125,000 years old have been found in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10811218_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10811218-55">[53]</a></sup> indicating the diet of early humans included seafood obtained by <a href="/wiki/Beachcombing" title="Beachcombing">beachcombing</a>.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Dating:_pre-or_post-Toba">Dating: pre-or post-Toba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dating: pre-or post-Toba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba catastrophe theory</a></div>
<p>The dating of the Southern Dispersal is a matter of dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012-39">[37]</a></sup> It may have happened either pre- or post-Toba, a catastrophic volcanic eruption that took place between 69,000 and 77,000 years ago at the site of present-day <a href="/wiki/Lake_Toba" title="Lake Toba">Lake Toba</a>. Stone tools discovered below the layers of ash disposed in India may point to a pre-Toba dispersal but the source of the tools is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012-39">[37]</a></sup> An indication for post-Toba is haplo-group L3, that originated before the dispersal of humans out of Africa and can be dated to 60,000–70,000 years ago, "suggesting that humanity left Africa a few thousand years after Toba".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAppenzeller2012-39">[37]</a></sup> New research showing slower than expected genetic mutations in human DNA was published in 2012, indicating a revised dating for the migration to between 90,000 and 130,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ourtruedawn_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ourtruedawn-56">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Peopling_of_South-Asia_and_Australia">Peopling of South-Asia and Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Peopling of South-Asia and Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>It is thought that Australia was colonised around 65,000-50,000 years ago. As of 2017, the earliest evidence of humans in Australia is at least 65,000 years old,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarkson2017_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clarkson2017-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StFleur2017_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-StFleur2017-10">[10]</a></sup> while McChesney stated that</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...genetic evidence suggests that a small band with the marker M168 migrated out of Africa along the coasts of the Arabian Peninsula and India, through Indonesia, and reached Australia very early, between 60,000 and 50,000 years ago. This very early migration into Australia is also supported by Rasmussen et al. (2011).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung_McChesney2015_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung_McChesney2015-13">[11]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Peopling_of_Europe">Peopling of Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Peopling of Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Toba">Pre-Toba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Pre-Toba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>According to <a href="#CITEREFMeredith2011">Meredith (2011)</a>, members of one branch of <i>Homo sapiens</i> left Africa at some point between 125,000 and 60,000 years ago and replaced other populations of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Toba">Post-Toba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Post-Toba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toba_supereruption" title="Toba supereruption">Toba supereruption</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="#CITEREFMacaulay_et_al.2005">Macaulay et al. (2005)</a>, an early offshoot from the southern disposal with haplogroup N, followed the Nile from East Africa, heading northwards and crossing into <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinai" title="Sinai">Sinai</a>. This group then branched, some moving into Europe and others heading east into Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macaulay2005_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Macaulay2005-14">[12]</a></sup> This hypothesis is supported by the relatively late date of the arrival of modern humans in Europe as well as by archaeological and DNA evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macaulay2005_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-Macaulay2005-14">[12]</a></sup> Based on an analysis of 55 human mitochondrial genomes (mtDNAs) of hunter-gatherers, <a href="#CITEREFPosth_et_al.2016">Posth et al. (2016)</a> argue for a "rapid single dispersal of all non-Africans less than 55,000 years ago."</p>
<p>Several authors have argued for multiple dispersals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Posth2016_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Posth2016-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroucutt_et_al.2015_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroucutt_et_al.2015-57">[55]</a></sup> <a href="#CITEREFWells2003">Wells (2003)</a> describes two waves of migration, first the southern coastal route, then a northern migration into Europe at circa 45,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McChesney2015_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-McChesney2015-58">[note 3]</a></sup> This possibility is ruled out by <a href="#CITEREFMacaulay_et_al.2005">Macaulay et al. (2005)</a> and <a href="#CITEREFPosth_et_al.2016">Posth et al. (2016)</a>, arguing for a single coastal dispersal, with an early offshoot into Europe.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_reconstruction">Genetic reconstruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Genetic reconstruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitochondrial_haplogroups">Mitochondrial haplogroups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mitochondrial haplogroups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Within_Africa">Within Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Within Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">Most recent common ancestor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeogenetics" title="Archaeogenetics">Archaeogenetics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:African_Mitochondrial_descent.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="387" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/African_Mitochondrial_descent.PNG/220px-African_Mitochondrial_descent.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/African_Mitochondrial_descent.PNG/330px-African_Mitochondrial_descent.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/African_Mitochondrial_descent.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:African_Mitochondrial_descent.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of early diversification of modern humans according to <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial</a> <a href="/wiki/Population_genetics" title="Population genetics">population genetics</a> <i>(see: <a href="/wiki/Macro-haplogroup_L_(mtDNA)" title="Macro-haplogroup L (mtDNA)">Haplogroup L</a>)</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first lineage to branch off from <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_Eve" title="Mitochondrial Eve">Mitochondrial Eve</a> is <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L0_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L0 (mtDNA)">L0</a>. This haplogroup is found in high proportions among the <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San</a> of Southern Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Sandawe_people" title="Sandawe people">Sandawe</a> of East Africa. It is also found among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mbuti" title="Mbuti">Mbuti</a> people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17194802_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17194802-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10739760_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10739760-60">[57]</a></sup> These groups branched off early in human history and have remained relatively genetically isolated since then. <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L1_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L1 (mtDNA)">Haplogroups L1</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L2_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L2 (mtDNA)">L2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L3_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L3 (mtDNA)">L3</a> are descendants of L1-6 and are largely confined to Africa. The macro haplogroups <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">M</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup N (mtDNA)">N</a>, which are the lineages of the rest of the world outside Africa, descend from L3. L3 is about 84,000 years old and haplogroup M and N are about 63,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macaulay2005_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-Macaulay2005-14">[12]</a></sup> The relationship between such gene trees and demographic history is still debated when applied to dispersals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroucutt_et_al.2015_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroucutt_et_al.2015-57">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of all the lineages present in Africa, only the female descendants of one lineage, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L3_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L3 (mtDNA)">mtDNA haplogroup L3</a>, are found outside Africa. If there had been several migrations, one would expect descendants of more than one lineage to be found. L3's female descendants, the <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">M</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup N (mtDNA)">N</a> haplogroup lineages, are found in very low frequencies in Africa (although <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)#Haplogroup_M1" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">haplogroup M1</a> populations are very ancient and diversified in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">North-east Africa</a>) and appear to be more recent arrivals. A possible explanation is that these mutations occurred in East Africa shortly before the exodus and became the dominant haplogroups after the departure through the <a href="/wiki/Founder_effect" title="Founder effect">founder effect</a>. Alternatively, the mutations may have arisen shortly afterwards.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Route_and_haplogroups_M_and_N">Southern Route and haplogroups M and N</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Southern Route and haplogroups M and N">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Results from mtDNA collected from aboriginal Malaysians called <a href="/wiki/Orang_Asli" title="Orang Asli">Orang Asli</a> and the creation of a phylogentic tree indicate that the hapologroups M and N share characteristics with original African groups from approximately 85,000 years ago and share characteristics with sub-haplogroups among coastal south-east Asian regions, such as Australasia, the Indian subcontinent and throughout continental Asia, which had dispersed and separated from its African origins approximately 65,000 years ago. This southern coastal dispersion would have occurred before the dispersion through the Levant approximately 45,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macaulay2005_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-Macaulay2005-14">[12]</a></sup> This hypothesis attempts to explain why haplogroup N is predominant in Europe and why haplogroup M is absent in Europe. Evidence of the coastal migration is thought to have been destroyed by the rise in sea levels during the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> epoch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> Alternatively, a small European founder population that had expressed haplogroup M and N at first, could have lost haplogroup M through random <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">genetic drift</a> resulting from a <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">bottleneck</a> (i.e. a <a href="/wiki/Founder_effect" title="Founder effect">founder effect</a>).</p>
<p>The group that crossed the Red Sea travelled along the coastal route around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> until reaching India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15339343_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15339343-53">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">Haplogroup M</a> is found in high frequencies along the southern coastal regions of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and India and it has the greatest diversity in India, indicating that it is here where the mutation may have occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15339343_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15339343-53">[51]</a></sup> Sixty percent of the Indian population belong to <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">Haplogroup M</a>. The indigenous people of the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Islands" title="Andaman Islands">Andaman Islands</a> also belong to the M lineage. The Andamanese are thought to be offshoots of some of the earliest inhabitants in Asia because of their long isolation from the mainland. They are evidence of the coastal route of early settlers that extends from India to <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and Indonesia all the way to <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>. Since M is found in high frequencies in highlanders from New Guinea and the Andamanese and New Guineans have dark skin and <a href="/wiki/Hair" title="Hair">Afro-textured hair</a>, some scientists think they are all part of the same wave of migrants who departed across the Red Sea ~60,000 years ago in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Coastal_Migration" title="Great Coastal Migration">Great Coastal Migration</a>. The proportion of haplogroup M increases eastwards from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> to India; in eastern India, M outnumbers N by a ratio of 3:1. Crossing into Southeast Asia, haplogroup N (mostly in the form of derivatives of its R subclade) reappears as the predominant lineage.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> M is predominant in East Asia, but amongst <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a>, N is the more common lineage.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This haphazard distribution of Haplogroup N from Europe to Australia can be explained by <a href="/wiki/Founder_effect" title="Founder effect">founder effects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">population bottlenecks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12840039_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12840039-62">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autosomal_DNA">Autosomal DNA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Autosomal DNA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Human_genetic_variation" title="Human genetic variation">Human genetic variation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_genetic_clustering" title="Human genetic clustering">Human genetic clustering</a></div>
<p>A 2002 study of African, European and Asian populations, found greater genetic diversity among Africans than among Eurasians, and that genetic diversity among Eurasians is largely a subset of that among Africans, supporting the out of Africa model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> A large study by Coop <i>et al</i>. (2009) found evidence for <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> in <a href="/wiki/Autosome" title="Autosome">autosomal</a> DNA outside of Africa. The study distinguishes non-African sweeps (notably <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell_factor" title="Stem cell factor">KITLG</a> variants associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_color" title="Skin color">skin color</a>), West-Eurasian sweeps (<a href="/wiki/SLC24A5" title="SLC24A5">SLC24A5</a>) and East-Asian sweeps (<a href="/wiki/Melanocortin_1_receptor" title="Melanocortin 1 receptor">MC1R</a>, relevant to skin color). Based on this evidence, the study concluded that human populations encountered novel selective pressures as they expanded out of Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coop2009_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-coop2009-64">[61]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MC1R" title="MC1R">MC1R</a> and its relation to skin color had already been discussed by <a href="#CITEREFLiu.2C_Harding_et_al.2000">Liu, Harding et al. (2000)</a>, p. 135. According to this study, Papua New Guineans continued to be exposed to selection for dark skin color so that, although these groups are distinct from Africans in other places, the allele for dark skin color shared by contemporary Africans, Andamanese and New Guineans is an archaism. <a href="#CITEREFEndicott_et_al.2003">Endicott et al. (2003)</a> suggest <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>. A 2014 study by Gurdasani et al. indicate that higher genetic diversity in Africa was caused by relatively recent Eurasian migrations <i>into</i> Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathogenic_load">Pathogenic load</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pathogenic load">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another promising route towards reconstructing human genetic genealogy is via the <a href="/wiki/JC_virus" title="JC virus">JC virus</a> (JCV), a type of human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyomavirus" title="Polyomavirus">polyomavirus</a> which is carried by 70–90 percent of humans and which is usually transmitted vertically, from parents to offspring, suggesting codivergence with human populations. For this reason, JCV has been used as a genetic marker for human evolution and migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> This method does not appear to be reliable for the migration out of Africa, in contrast to human genetics, JCV strains associated with African populations are not basal. From this <a href="#CITEREFShackelton_et_al.2006">Shackelton et al. (2006)</a> conclude that either a basal African strain of JCV has become extinct or that the original infection with JCV post-dates the migration from Africa.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Admixture_of_archaic_and_modern_humans">Admixture of archaic and modern humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Admixture of archaic and modern humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Archaic human admixture with modern humans</a></div>
<p>Evidence for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human" title="Archaic human">archaic human</a> species (descended from <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">Homo heidelbergensis</a></i>) having <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">interbred</a> with modern humans outside of Africa, was discovered in the 2010s. This concerns primarily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthal</a> admixture in all modern populations except for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africans" title="Sub-Saharan Africans">Sub-Saharan Africans</a> but evidence has also been presented for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denisova_hominin" title="Denisova hominin">Denisova hominin</a> admixture in <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a> (i.e. in <a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Australians" title="Aboriginal Australians">Aboriginal Australians</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negritos" title="Negritos">Negritos</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> The rate of admixture has been found to be relatively low (1–10%), and the result is still open to revision, as the presence of archaic markers has also been suggested as being due to shared ancestral traits rather than interbreeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, evidence was presented for archaic admixture in some Sub-Saharan African populations (<a href="/wiki/Aka_people" title="Aka people">Biaka</a> Pygmies and <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San</a>), derived from archaic hominins that broke away from the modern human lineage around 700,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamgenev_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-hamgenev-69">[66]</a></sup> After a survey for the introgressive haplotypes across Sub-Saharan populations, it was suggested that the admixture event happened with archaic hominins that possibly once inhabited Central Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamgenev_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-hamgenev-69">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stone_tools">Stone tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Stone tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Aterian" title="Aterian">Aterian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baradostian" title="Baradostian">Baradostian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Microlith" title="Microlith">Microlith</a></div>
<p>In addition to genetic analysis, Petraglia <i>et al.</i> also examines the small stone tools (<a href="/wiki/Microlith" title="Microlith">microlithic</a> materials) from Indian subcontinent and explains the expansion of population based on the reconstruction of paleoenvironment. He proposed that the stone tools could be dated to 35 ka in South Asia, and the new technology might be influenced by environmental change and population pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_theory">History of the theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: History of the theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_paleoanthropology">Classical paleoanthropology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Classical paleoanthropology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_human_evolution" title="Timeline of human evolution">Timeline of human evolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology#History_of_paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">Paleoanthropology § History_of_paleoanthropology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huxley_-_Mans_Place_in_Nature.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="1103" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Huxley_-_Mans_Place_in_Nature.jpg/220px-Huxley_-_Mans_Place_in_Nature.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Huxley_-_Mans_Place_in_Nature.jpg/330px-Huxley_-_Mans_Place_in_Nature.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Huxley_-_Mans_Place_in_Nature.jpg/440px-Huxley_-_Mans_Place_in_Nature.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Huxley_-_Mans_Place_in_Nature.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The frontispiece to Huxley's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evidence_as_to_Man%27s_Place_in_Nature" title="Evidence as to Man's Place in Nature">Evidence as to Man's Place in Nature</a></i> (1863): the image compares the skeleton of a human to other apes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The cladistic relationship of humans with the African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apes" title="Apes">apes</a> was suggested by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> after studying the behaviour of African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apes" title="Apes">apes</a>, one of which was displayed at the <a href="/wiki/London_Zoo" title="London Zoo">London Zoo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lafreniere2010_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lafreniere2010-71">[68]</a></sup> The anatomist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Huxley" title="Thomas Huxley">Thomas Huxley</a> had also supported the hypothesis and suggested that African apes have a close evolutionary relationship with humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-470-01315-X_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-470-01315-X-72">[69]</a></sup> These views were opposed by the German biologist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a>, who was a proponent of the <a href="/wiki/Out_of_Asia_theory" title="Out of Asia theory">Out of Asia theory</a>. Haeckel argued that humans were more closely related to the primates of South-east Asia and rejected Darwin's African hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-520-24827-9_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-520-24827-9-73">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn1-85109-418-0_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn1-85109-418-0-74">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descent_of_Man" title="Descent of Man">Descent of Man</a></i>, Darwin speculated that humans had descended from apes, which still had small brains but walked upright, freeing their hands for uses which favoured intelligence; he thought such apes were African:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In each great region of the world the living <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammals" title="Mammals">mammals</a> are closely related to the extinct species of the same region. It is, therefore, probable that Africa was formerly inhabited by extinct apes closely allied to the <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorilla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a>; and as these two species are now man's nearest allies, it is somewhat more probable that our early progenitors lived on the African continent than elsewhere. But it is useless to speculate on this subject, for an ape nearly as large as a man, namely the <a href="/wiki/Dryopithecus" title="Dryopithecus">Dryopithecus</a> of Lartet, which was closely allied to the anthropomorphous <a href="/wiki/Hylobates" title="Hylobates">Hylobates</a>, existed in Europe during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Miocene" title="Upper Miocene">Upper Miocene</a> period; and since so remote a period the earth has certainly undergone many great revolutions, and there has been ample time for migration on the largest scale.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Charles Darwin, Descent of Man<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1871 there were hardly any human fossils of ancient hominins available. Almost fifty years later, Darwin's speculation was supported when anthropologists began finding fossils of ancient small-brained hominins in several areas of Africa (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_hominina_fossils" title="List of hominina fossils">list of hominina fossils</a>). The hypothesis of <i>recent</i> (as opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out_of_Africa_I" title="Out of Africa I">archaic</a>) African origin developed in the 20th century. The "Recent African origin" of modern humans means "single origin" (monogenism) and has been used in various contexts as an antonym to polygenism. The debate in anthropology had swung in favour of monogenism by the mid-20th century. Isolated proponents of polygenism held forth in the mid-20th century, such as <a href="/wiki/Carleton_S._Coon" title="Carleton S. Coon">Carleton Coon</a>, who thought as late as 1962 that <i>H. sapiens</i> arose five times from <i>H. erectus</i> in five places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson_2001_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson_2001-76">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiregional_origin_hypothesis">Multiregional origin hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Multiregional origin hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multiregional_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Multiregional origin of modern humans">Multiregional origin of modern humans</a></div>
<p>The historical alternative to the recent origin model is the <a href="/wiki/Multiregional_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Multiregional origin of modern humans">multiregional origin of modern humans</a>, initially proposed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milford_Wolpoff" title="Milford Wolpoff">Milford Wolpoff</a> in the 1980s. This view proposes that the derivation of anatomically modern human populations from <i>H. erectus</i> at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> 1.8 million years BP, has taken place within a continuous world population. The hypothesis necessarily rejects the assumption of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_barrier" title="Species barrier">infertility barrier</a> between ancient Eurasian and African populations of <i>Homo</i>. The hypothesis was controversially debated during the late 1980s and the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> The now-current terminology of "recent-origin" and "Out of Africa" became current in the context of this debate in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup> Originally seen as an antithetical alternative to the recent origin model, the multiregional hypothesis in its original "strong" form is obsolete, while its various modified weaker variants have become variants of a view of "recent origin" combined with <a href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">archaic admixture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> Stringer (2014) distinguishes the original or "classic" Multiregional model as having existed from 1984 (its formulation) until 2003, to a "weak" post-2003 variant that has "shifted close to that of the Assimilation Model".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Allan_Wilson" title="Allan Wilson">Allan Wilson</a> together with <a href="/wiki/Rebecca_L._Cann" title="Rebecca L. Cann">Rebecca L. Cann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Stoneking" title="Mark Stoneking">Mark Stoneking</a> worked on genetic dating of the matrilineal most recent common ancestor of modern human populations (dubbed "<a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_Eve" title="Mitochondrial Eve">Mitochondrial Eve</a>"). To identify informative <a href="/wiki/Genetic_marker" title="Genetic marker">genetic markers</a> for tracking human evolutionary history, Wilson concentrated on <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (mtDNA), passed from mother to child. This DNA material mutates quickly, making it easy to plot changes over relatively short times. With his discovery that human mtDNA is genetically much less diverse than chimpanzee mtDNA, Wilson concluded that modern human populations had diverged recently from a single population while older human species such as <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> had become extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-url_Allan_Wilson_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-url_Allan_Wilson-82">[79]</a></sup> With the advent of <a href="/wiki/Archaeogenetics" title="Archaeogenetics">archaeogenetics</a> in the 1990s, the dating of mitochondrial and <a href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup">Y-chromosomal haplogroups</a> became possible with some confidence. By 1999, estimates ranged around 150,000 years for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mt-MRCA" title="Mt-MRCA">mt-MRCA</a> and 60,000 to 70,000 years for the migration out of Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 2000–2003, there was controversy about the mitochondrial DNA of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mungo_Man" title="Mungo Man">Mungo Man 3</a>" (LM3) and its possible bearing on the multiregional hypothesis. LM3 was found to have more than the expected number of sequence differences when compared to modern human DNA (<a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Reference_Sequence" title="Cambridge Reference Sequence">CRS</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adcock_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adcock-84">[81]</a></sup> Comparison of the mitochondrial DNA with that of ancient and modern aborigines, led to the conclusion that Mungo Man fell outside the range of genetic variation seen in Aboriginal Australians and was used to support the multiregional origin hypothesis. A reanalysis on LM3 and other ancient specimens from the area published in 2016, showed it to be akin to modern Aboriginal Australian sequences, inconsistent with the results of the earlier study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Informational notes</b></p>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 35em; -webkit-column-width: 35em; column-width: 35em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The earliest evidence of humans in Australia is at least 65,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarkson2017_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarkson2017-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StFleur2017_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-StFleur2017-10">[10]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-McChesney2015_short-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-McChesney2015_short_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">McChesney: "...genetic evidence suggests that a small band with the marker M168 migrated out of Africa along the coasts of the Arabian Peninsula and India, through Indonesia, and reached Australia very early, between 60,000 and 50,000 years ago. This very early migration into Australia is also supported by Rasmussen et al. (2011).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-McChesney2015-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-McChesney2015_58-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kay Young McChesney: "Wells (2003) divided the descendants of men who left Africa into a genealogical tree with 11 lineages. Each genetic marker represents a single-point mutation (SNP) at a specific place in the genome. First, genetic evidence suggests that a small band with the marker M168 migrated out of Africa along the coasts of the Arabian Peninsula and India, through Indonesia, and reached Australia very early, between 60,000 and 50,000 years ago. This very early migration into Australia is also supported by Rasmussen et al. (2011). Second, a group bearing the marker M89 moved out of northeastern Africa into the Middle East 45,000 years ago. From there, the M89 group split into two groups. One group that developed the marker M9 went into Asia about 40,000 years ago. The Asian (M9) group split three ways: into Central Asia (M45), 35,000 years ago; into India (M20), 30,000 years ago; and into China (M122), 10,000 years ago. The Central Asian (M45) group split into two groups: toward Europe (M173), 30,000 years ago and toward Siberia (M242), 20,000 years ago. Finally, the Siberian group (M242) went on to populate North and South America (M3), about 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung_McChesney2015_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung_McChesney2015-13">[11]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
